      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Employed_research_techniques">
       Employed research techniques
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_photosynthesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Employed research techniques">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Research in artificial photosynthesis is necessarily a multidisciplinary field, requiring a multitude of different expertise.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Magnuson_10-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Magnuson-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some techniques employed in making and investigating catalysts and solar cells include:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Organic_synthesis" title="Organic synthesis">
        Organic
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Inorganic_chemistry" title="Inorganic chemistry">
        inorganic
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synthesis" title="Chemical synthesis">
        chemical synthesis
       </a>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Electrochemistry" title="Electrochemistry">
        Electrochemistry
       </a>
       methods, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Photoelectrochemistry" title="Photoelectrochemistry">
        photoelectrochemistry
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Cyclic_voltammetry" title="Cyclic voltammetry">
        cyclic voltammetry
       </a>
       , electrochemical impedance spectroscopy
       <a href="/wiki/Dielectric_spectroscopy" title="Dielectric spectroscopy">
        Dielectric spectroscopy
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Bulk_electrolysis" title="Bulk electrolysis">
        bulk electrolysis
       </a>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       Spectroscopic methods:
       <ul>
        <li>
         fast techniques, such as
         <a href="/wiki/Time-resolved_spectroscopy" title="Time-resolved spectroscopy">
          time-resolved spectroscopy
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Ultrafast_laser_spectroscopy" title="Ultrafast laser spectroscopy">
          ultrafast laser spectroscopy
         </a>
         ;
        </li>
        <li>
         magnetic resonance spectroscopies, such as
         <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_magnetic_resonance" title="Nuclear magnetic resonance">
          nuclear magnetic resonance
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Electron_paramagnetic_resonance" title="Electron paramagnetic resonance">
          electron paramagnetic resonance
         </a>
         ;
        </li>
        <li>
         X-ray spectroscopy methods, including x-ray absorption such as
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/XANES" title="XANES">
          XANES
         </a>
         and
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EXAFS" title="EXAFS">
          EXAFS
         </a>
         , but also x-ray emission.
        </li>
       </ul>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Crystallography" title="Crystallography">
        Crystallography
       </a>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">
        Molecular biology
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Microbiology" title="Microbiology">
        microbiology
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_biology" title="Synthetic biology">
        synthetic biology
       </a>
       methodologies.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Advantages.2C_disadvantages.2C_and_efficiency">
       Advantages, disadvantages, and efficiency
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Artificial_photosynthesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Advantages, disadvantages, and efficiency">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Advantages of solar fuel production through artificial photosynthesis include:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       The solar energy can be immediately converted and stored. In
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photovoltaic" title="Photovoltaic">
        photovoltaic
       </a>
       cells, sunlight is converted into electricity and then converted again into chemical energy for storage, with some necessary loss of energy associated with the second conversion.
      </li>
      <li>
       The byproducts of these reactions are environmentally friendly. Artificially photosynthesized fuel would be a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon-neutral" title="Carbon-neutral">
        carbon-neutral
       </a>
       source of energy, which could be used for transportation or homes.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      Disadvantages include:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Materials used for artificial photosynthesis often corrode in water, so they may be less stable than
       <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaics" title="Photovoltaics">
        photovoltaics
       </a>
       over long periods of time. Most hydrogen catalysts are very sensitive to oxygen, being inactivated or degraded in its presence; also, photodamage may occur over time.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Andreiadis_9-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-Andreiadis-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krassen_72-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Krassen-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       The overall cost is not yet advantageous enough to compete with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" title="Fossil fuels">
        fossil fuels
       </a>
       as a commercially viable source of energy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economist1_3-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-economist1-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      A concern usually addressed in catalyst design is efficiency, in particular how much of the incident light can be used in a system in practice. This is comparable with
      <a href="/wiki/Photosynthetic_efficiency" title="Photosynthetic efficiency">
       photosynthetic efficiency
      </a>
      , where light-to-chemical-energy conversion is measured. Photosynthetic organisms are able to collect about 50% of incident solar radiation, however the theoretical limit of photosynthetic efficiency is 4.6 and 6.0% for C3 and C4 plants respectively.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blankenship_78-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Blankenship-78">
        [78]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In reality, the efficiency of photosynthesis is much lower and is usually below 1%, with some exceptions such as
      <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">
       sugarcane
      </a>
      in tropical climate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In contrast, the highest reported efficiency for artificial photosynthesis lab prototypes is 22.4%.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
       <a href="#cite_note-80">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, plants are efficient in using CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      at atmospheric concentrations, something that artificial catalysts still cannot perform.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biello_81-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Biello-81">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
